💬 Response (25,680 in / 1,873 out)
Congratulations on your upcoming arrival! 🎉 Here is a clear, step-by-step guide to everything you need to do and when:

---

## 🗓️ RIGHT NOW (You have 6 weeks left — act immediately!)

### 1. Notify Your Employer & Claim Maternity Leave/Pay
⚠️ **This is urgent — you are very close to the deadline.**

You must tell your employer **at least 15 weeks before your due date** when the baby is due and when you want your maternity leave to start. At 6 weeks out, you are already past this point, so notify your employer **as soon as possible**.

- **Statutory Maternity Leave** is available to all employees regardless of how long you've worked there or how many hours you work.
- **Statutory Maternity Pay (SMP)** requires you to have worked for your employer continuously for at least **26 weeks** up to the 15th week before your due date, and earn at least **£125/week** on average.
- You'll need to provide your **MATB1 certificate** (given to you by your midwife or GP no more than 20 weeks before your due date) as proof of pregnancy.
- If you don't qualify for SMP, your employer must give you form **SMP1** and you may be eligible for **Maternity Allowance** instead.

👉 [Maternity pay and leave – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave)

---

### 2. Apply for the Sure Start Maternity Grant (if eligible)
A **one-off £500 payment** to help with the costs of a new baby. Since this is your first child, you may qualify — but **you must claim from 11 weeks before your due date up to 6 months after the birth**, so you can apply now.

**Eligibility:** You (or your partner) must already be receiving certain benefits, such as Universal Credit, Income Support, or Child Tax Credit.

👉 [Sure Start Maternity Grant – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant)

---

## 🏥 AFTER THE BIRTH

### 3. Register the Birth
**Deadline: Within 42 days of the birth** (in England, Wales & Northern Ireland).

You can do this at:
- The **local register office** for the area where the baby was born, OR
- **The hospital**, before the mother leaves (ask the hospital if this is available)

You'll need to bring ID (passport, birth certificate, driving licence, etc.) and know details such as the baby's name, date/place of birth, and parents' details. Take your child's **red book** (personal child health record) too, as some registrars may ask for it.

👉 [Register a birth – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth)

---

### 4. Claim Child Benefit
You can claim **48 hours after registering the birth**. Child Benefit can be backdated up to **3 months**, so don't delay too long.

- You'll need your **child's birth certificate**, your bank details, and your National Insurance number.
- You can claim online, by post, or by phone.
- **Important:** Only one person in the household can claim for a child. Whoever claims also receives **National Insurance credits** towards their State Pension — worth considering if you're not working.
- If either you or your partner earns over **£60,000/year**, you may be subject to the **High Income Child Benefit Charge** — but it can still be worth claiming for the NI credits.

👉 [Child Benefit: Make a claim – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit/how-to-claim)

---

## 📋 Summary Timeline

| When | What to do |
|---|---|
| **Now (immediately)** | Notify employer of maternity leave & pay |
| **Now (if eligible)** | Apply for Sure Start Maternity Grant |
| **Within 42 days of birth** | Register the birth at a register office |
| **48 hours after registering** | Claim Child Benefit online |

---

## 💡 Other things to consider
- **Shared Parental Leave & Pay** – if your partner is also employed, you may be able to share some of your leave with them.
- **Free NHS dental treatment & prescriptions** – you're entitled to these during pregnancy and for 12 months after the birth (your midwife can give you a FW8 form for an exemption certificate).
- **Healthy Start vouchers** – if you're on certain benefits, you may qualify for vouchers to help buy food and milk.

Would you like more detail on any of these steps?
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 4/5 · Accuracy: 4/5 · Sequencing: 4/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 4/5 ●●●●○
Covers birth registration, Child Benefit, SMP, Maternity Allowance, Shared Parental Leave, NHS maternity exemption (prescriptions/dental), and Sure Start Maternity Grant; however, it omits Universal Credit maternity considerations, Tax-Free Childcare, and free childcare entitlements for 2-year-olds which the reference checklist flags as conditional/proactive services.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
Most details are correct, including the 42-day registration deadline, Child Benefit backdating, SMP qualifying conditions, and the £60,000 HICBC threshold; however, the claim that you can register at the hospital before leaving is not standard practice in most areas (it's rare and largely historical), and the statement that Child Benefit can be claimed '48 hours after registering the birth' is not an official rule — you need the birth certificate but there's no mandated 48-hour wait.
Sequencing 4/5 ●●●●○
The sequencing is logical — employer notification first as urgent, then post-birth registration and Child Benefit — though the urgency framing around the 15-week employer notification deadline could be slightly misleading since at 6 weeks before the due date the statutory deadline has already passed, making it a catch-up action rather than a deadline to meet.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
Exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, a summary timeline table, emoji visual cues, direct GOV.UK links, and a logical flow that would be very easy for a first-time parent to follow.
Notable omissions: hmrc-tax-free-childcare, la-free-childcare-2yr, dwp-universal-credit
Notable errors: The '48 hours after registering' claim for Child Benefit is not an official requirement — you simply need the birth certificate, Hospital-based birth registration is presented as commonly available but is rare in practice
TREATMENT

💬 Response (18,079 in / 1,617 out)
Congratulations on your first baby! 🎉 Here's a clear, phased guide to everything you need to do and when — covering the most important services for you.

---

## 🤰 Right Now (Before the Birth — 6 Weeks to Go)

### 1. Get Your Maternity Exemption Certificate (MatEx)
Ask your **midwife or GP** to issue this. It gives you **free NHS prescriptions and free dental treatment** throughout your pregnancy and for **12 months after the birth**. Don't wait — you're entitled to this right now.
👉 [nhs.uk — free prescriptions in pregnancy](https://www.nhs.uk/pregnancy/finding-out/free-nhs-prescriptions-and-dental-care/)

### 2. Sort Your Maternity/Paternity Pay
- **Statutory Maternity Pay (SMP):** If you've been employed by the same employer for 26+ weeks by the 15th week before your due date, notify your employer now. SMP pays for up to 39 weeks.
- **Maternity Allowance:** If you're self-employed, recently changed jobs, or don't qualify for SMP, claim this from the DWP instead — also up to 39 weeks.
- **Statutory Paternity Pay:** If your partner is taking paternity leave, they must arrange this **before the baby is 8 weeks old** — so it's worth your partner notifying their employer now.
👉 [gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-pay-leave) | [gov.uk/maternity-allowance](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-allowance)

### 3. Sure Start Maternity Grant (if on qualifying benefits)
If you're on Universal Credit, Income Support, or another qualifying benefit, you can apply for a **one-off £500 grant** from as early as **11 weeks before your due date** — so you can apply right now! This is usually only available for your **first child**, and you have up to 3 months after the birth to claim.
👉 [gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant)

---

## 👶 Within the First 6 Weeks After Birth

### 4. Register the Birth ⚠️ Deadline: 42 days
This is your most important legal obligation. You must register your baby at your **local register office within 42 days**. This unlocks almost everything else — Child Benefit, free childcare, and more.
👉 [gov.uk/register-birth](https://www.gov.uk/register-birth)

### 5. Claim Child Benefit
Apply as soon as the birth is registered. It's **backdatable by up to 3 months**, so don't delay. It's available to everyone responsible for a child under 16 — but note the **High Income Child Benefit Charge** applies if either parent earns over £60,000.
👉 [gov.uk/child-benefit](https://www.gov.uk/child-benefit)

---

## 🍼 In the Coming Months

### 6. Shared Parental Leave (if applicable)
If both you and your partner want to share the remaining leave and pay, you can split up to **50 weeks of leave and 37 weeks of pay** between you. Both employers need to be notified. Plan this early as it requires advance notice.
👉 [gov.uk/shared-parental-leave-and-pay](https://www.gov.uk/shared-parental-leave-and-pay)

### 7. Tax-Free Childcare Account
When you return to work, open a **Tax-Free Childcare account** — the government tops up every £8 you save with £2 (up to £500 per quarter). You must be working and earning at least the National Minimum Wage for 16 hours.
👉 [gov.uk/tax-free-childcare](https://www.gov.uk/tax-free-childcare)

### 8. Free Childcare — 15 Hours (from 9 months old)
From the term after your baby turns **9 months old**, they're entitled to **15 hours/week of free childcare**. Apply via your Tax-Free Childcare account. No income test — this is universal.
👉 [gov.uk/help-paying-childcare](https://www.gov.uk/help-paying-childcare/free-childcare-and-education-for-2-to-4-year-olds)

### 9. Free Childcare — 30 Hours (from age 3–4)
When your child turns 3, if both parents are working at least 16 hours/week at minimum wage (and neither earns over £100,000), you can get **30 hours/week** of free childcare.

---

## 💰 If You're on a Low Income

| Benefit | What it is |
|---|---|
| **Universal Credit** | Main benefit for low income/out of work — report your new baby as a change of circumstances |
| **Healthy Start vouchers** | Up to £8.50/week on a prepaid card for food & milk (if on qualifying benefits, 10+ weeks pregnant) |
| **NHS Low Income Scheme (HC2)** | Free prescriptions, dental & sight tests beyond the maternity exemption period |

---

## 📋 Quick Summary Timeline

| When | Action |
|---|---|
| **Now** | Get Maternity Exemption Certificate; notify employer of maternity/paternity leave; apply for Sure Start Grant (if eligible) |
| **Within 42 days of birth** | ⚠️ Register the birth |
| **After registration** | Claim Child Benefit; apply for Sure Start Grant if not done yet |
| **Partner within 8 weeks** | Arrange Statutory Paternity Pay |
| **From 9 months** | Apply for 15 hours free childcare |
| **Age 3–4** | Apply for 30 hours free childcare (if eligible) |

---

Completeness 5/5 ●●●●●
The response covers all essential services (birth registration, Child Benefit, NHS maternity exemption), all conditional services (SMP, Maternity Allowance, Universal Credit, Tax-Free Childcare), and proactive services (Shared Parental Leave, free childcare entitlements), plus extras like Sure Start Grant, Healthy Start vouchers, and paternity pay.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
Almost all details are correct, though the claim that Child Benefit is 'backdatable by up to 3 months' is slightly misleading — it can be backdated up to 3 months but only if the child existed during that period, and the statement that the 15-hour universal entitlement from 9 months has 'no income test' is correct for the newer expanded entitlement but the framing could confuse with the existing 2-year-old entitlement for disadvantaged families which the reference checklist flags; also the High Income Child Benefit Charge threshold of £60,000 is correct for 2024-25 onwards.
Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
The phased timeline is logically ordered — pre-birth actions first, then immediate post-birth legal obligations, then medium-term and longer-term entitlements — with appropriate urgency markers on the 42-day registration deadline.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, a summary timeline table, links to official sources, and an offer to drill down further, making it highly actionable for a first-time parent.
Notable errors: The 15-hour free childcare from 9 months is described as universal with no income test, which is correct for the newer expanded entitlement but the response doesn't mention the separate 2-year-old entitlement for families on qualifying benefits (la-free-childcare-2yr from the reference checklist), which is a distinct scheme worth flagging.

💬 Response (23,960 in / 1,923 out)
Great, I now have all the detail I need. Here's a comprehensive breakdown of the additional benefits relevant to your specific situation — low income and a self-employed partner:

---

## 💰 Additional Benefits for Your Situation

---

### 1. 🤰 Maternity Allowance (for you — if you don't qualify for SMP)
Since your partner is self-employed (not your employer), this is most likely **your** route to maternity pay if you can't get Statutory Maternity Pay from your employer.

**You qualify for up to 39 weeks of Maternity Allowance if you have been:**
- **Employed** for at least **26 weeks** in the 66 weeks before your due date, earning at least **£30/week** in at least 13 of those weeks, OR
- **Registered as self-employed** for at least 26 weeks in the same period

**Payments can start** from 11 weeks before your due date — so you can apply **now** (you can apply from 26 weeks pregnant).

👉 [Maternity Allowance – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-allowance)

---

### 2. 🏠 Universal Credit (for your household)
As a low-income household, **Universal Credit is likely your most important benefit to claim**, and your partner being self-employed does **not** disqualify you. You can claim as a couple.

**You may be eligible if:**
- Your combined household savings are **£16,000 or less**
- You are both under State Pension age
- You live in the UK

**Important for self-employed partners:** Universal Credit applies a **Minimum Income Floor** for self-employed claimants (it assumes a minimum level of earnings), but this is **suspended for the first 12 months** of self-employment, and also during periods like maternity leave. It's worth checking your specific situation.

**Why this matters for your other benefits:** Being on Universal Credit also **unlocks** the Sure Start Maternity Grant and the Healthy Start scheme (see below), so **claim Universal Credit first** if you're not already on it.

👉 [Universal Credit – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit)
👉 [Universal Credit and self-employment – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/self-employment-and-universal-credit)

---

### 3. 💷 Sure Start Maternity Grant — £500 one-off payment
As mentioned previously, but now with the full eligibility detail:

**You qualify if:**
- This is your first child (no other children under 16), AND
- You **or your partner** are receiving one of: **Universal Credit**, Income Support, income-based JSA, income-related ESA, or Pension Credit

⚠️ **This is why claiming Universal Credit first is so important** — it's the gateway to this grant. If you're not already on a qualifying benefit, get your Universal Credit claim in as soon as possible, as you must claim the grant **within 11 weeks of your due date or 6 months after the birth**.

👉 [Sure Start Maternity Grant – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant)

---

### 4. 🥦 Healthy Start Vouchers
A scheme to help buy healthy food (milk, fruit, vegetables) and get free vitamins during pregnancy and after birth.

**You qualify if you are more than 10 weeks pregnant AND receiving:**
- Universal Credit (with no earnings, or earnings under £408/month after tax)
- Child Tax Credit, Working Tax Credit (with a disability element), or Income Support

Since you're on a low income, you may well qualify — especially once you're on Universal Credit.

**What you get:** A prepaid card loaded with money every 4 weeks to spend on milk, fruit, vegetables, and pulses. Amounts increase once the baby is born.

👉 [Healthy Start – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/healthy-start) / [Apply via NHS](https://www.healthystart.nhs.uk/)

---

### 5. 👶 Universal Credit Child Element
Once your baby is born and you're on Universal Credit, your payments will **automatically increase** with the addition of a **child element** for your new baby. Make sure you report the birth to Universal Credit promptly.

---

## 🔑 The Key Order of Steps for You

Because many benefits depend on each other, the order really matters:

```
1. CLAIM UNIVERSAL CREDIT NOW (if not already on it)
↓
2. APPLY FOR MATERNITY ALLOWANCE (you can do this now)
↓
3. APPLY FOR SURE START MATERNITY GRANT (now, using UC as qualifying benefit)
↓
4. APPLY FOR HEALTHY START VOUCHERS (now, using UC as qualifying benefit)
↓
5. AFTER BIRTH: Report baby to Universal Credit → child element added
6. AFTER BIRTH: Register birth → Claim Child Benefit
```

---

Use the **GOV.UK Benefits Calculator** to get a personalised estimate:
👉 [Benefits calculators – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/benefits-calculators)

This is especially useful for self-employed households as income can vary month to month.

💬 Response (22,906 in / 1,880 out)
Great news — I now have the full picture for your situation. Given you're on a **low income** and your **partner is self-employed**, here are the additional benefits and support you should look into, on top of what I mentioned earlier:

---

## 💷 Key Additional Benefits for Your Situation

### 1. Universal Credit — Your Most Important Claim
As a low-income household with a new baby, **Universal Credit (UC)** is likely your most valuable entitlement. It can include:
- A **standard household allowance**
- A **child element** (once your baby is born and registered)
- A **childcare element** — up to 85% of childcare costs covered when you return to work
- A **housing element** if you rent

> ⚠️ **Important for your partner:** As a self-employed person, your partner will be subject to the **Minimum Income Floor** after an initial 12-month "start-up period" — meaning UC assumes they earn at least the equivalent of 35 hours/week at minimum wage, even if they earn less. However, during the **first year of self-employment**, this doesn't apply, and there are also exemptions during pregnancy and for new parents. Report the baby as a change of circumstances within **1 month** of the birth.
👉 [gov.uk/universal-credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit)

---

### 2. Sure Start Maternity Grant — £500 One-Off Payment ⏰ Apply Now!
Since this is your **first child** and you're on a low income (likely qualifying for UC), you should apply for this **right now** — you can claim from **11 weeks before your due date**. Don't wait until after the birth.
👉 [gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant](https://www.gov.uk/sure-start-maternity-grant)

---

### 3. Maternity Allowance — For You (Instead of SMP)
Since your **partner is self-employed**, they won't qualify for Statutory Paternity Pay (which requires employment). But if **you** are also self-employed or don't qualify for SMP, you can claim **Maternity Allowance** from the DWP — up to **£184.03/week for 39 weeks** (or £27/week if you've done some work but don't meet the earnings test).
👉 [gov.uk/maternity-allowance](https://www.gov.uk/maternity-allowance)

> ⚠️ **Note on your partner:** As a self-employed person, your partner does **not** qualify for Statutory Paternity Pay, which requires 26 weeks of employment. They may still take time off, but without statutory pay unless they have other income protection.

---

### 4. Healthy Start Vouchers — Up to £8.50/week Right Now
If you're on UC (or other qualifying benefits) and **10+ weeks pregnant**, you can get a prepaid card loaded with **£8.50/week** to spend on fruit, vegetables, milk and infant formula. After the birth, it continues at **£4.25/week per child under 1**. Apply now — don't wait!
👉 [healthystart.nhs.uk](https://www.healthystart.nhs.uk/)

---

### 5. Free Childcare for Disadvantaged 2-Year-Olds
If you're still on a low income when your child turns 2, they'll be entitled to **15 hours/week of free childcare** — a year earlier than the universal entitlement. This is means-tested and linked to receiving UC or other income-related benefits.
👉 [gov.uk/help-with-childcare-costs/free-childcare-2-year-olds](https://www.gov.uk/help-with-childcare-costs/free-childcare-2-year-olds)

---

### 6. Council Tax Reduction
If you're on UC or a low income, apply to your **local council** for a reduction on your Council Tax bill. On a very low income, this can reduce your bill to **zero**. Apply as soon as your circumstances change (e.g. when you go on maternity leave).
👉 [gov.uk/apply-council-tax-reduction](https://www.gov.uk/apply-council-tax-reduction)

---

### 7. NHS Low Income Scheme (HC2 Certificate)
Once your **Maternity Exemption Certificate** expires (12 months after the birth), if you're still on a low income but not on UC, apply for an **HC2 certificate**. This gives you:
- Free NHS prescriptions
- Free NHS dental treatment
- Free NHS sight tests
- Help with travel to hospital

If you're on UC, you already get free prescriptions automatically.
👉 [gov.uk/nhs-low-income-scheme](https://www.gov.uk/nhs-low-income-scheme)

---

### 8. Help to Save — 50% Savings Bonus
If you're on UC, you can open a **Help to Save account** and earn a **50p government bonus for every £1 you save** (up to £50/month). You can save for up to 4 years and receive bonuses at the end of years 2 and 4 — a great way to build a financial buffer.
👉 [gov.uk/get-help-savings-low-income](https://www.gov.uk/get-help-savings-low-income)

---

### 9. Social Tariff Broadband
If you're on UC, you may be eligible for **discounted broadband** from major providers (BT, Virgin Media, Sky, etc.) at around **£12–20/month**. Worth checking with your current provider.
👉 [gov.uk/affordable-broadband](https://www.gov.uk/affordable-broadband)

---

## 📋 Priority Action List for Your Situation

| Priority | Action | When |
|---|---|---|
| 🔴 **Urgent** | Apply for **Sure Start Maternity Grant** (£500) | Right now — before the birth |
| 🔴 **Urgent** | Apply for **Healthy Start vouchers** | Right now if on UC |
| 🟠 **Soon** | Claim **Maternity Allowance** (if not getting SMP) | Now — up to 26 weeks before due date |
| 🟠 **Soon** | Claim/update **Universal Credit** | Now, and again within 1 month of birth |
| 🟡 **After birth** | Apply for **Council Tax Reduction** | After birth / change of circumstances |
| 🟡 **After birth** | Open a **Help to Save** account | Once on UC |
| 🟡 **After birth** | Check **Social Tariff Broadband** | Anytime on UC |
| 🟢 **Age 2** | Apply for **free childcare for disadvantaged 2-year-olds** | When child turns 2 (if still on low income) |

---

💬 Response (12,351 in / 626 out)
Based on the latest GOV.UK guidance, here's your answer:

---

## 👶 The Very First Thing: **Register the Birth**

Everything else — Child Benefit, Universal Credit child element, Sure Start Maternity Grant — flows from this one step, so it unlocks all the other support you're entitled to.

### 📍 Where to go
- Your **local register office** for the area where the baby was born, OR
- **The hospital**, before the mother leaves — ask the hospital if this option is available to you

### ⏰ When
- **Within 42 days of the birth** (this is a legal requirement)
- Try to do it as soon as you feel ready — the sooner it's done, the sooner you can claim Child Benefit and update Universal Credit

### 📋 What to bring
Take **at least one form of ID**, such as:
- Passport
- Birth certificate
- Driving licence
- Proof of address (utility bill or Council Tax bill)
- Marriage or civil partnership certificate (if applicable)

Also bring your baby's **red book** (personal child health record), as some registrars may ask for it.

### 📝 Information you'll need to know
- Baby's name, surname and sex
- Date and place of birth
- Both parents' names, surnames, addresses, dates and places of birth
- Both parents' jobs
- Mother's maiden surname
- Date of parents' marriage or civil partnership (if applicable)

---

### ➡️ What happens next, immediately after registering:

```
Register the birth
↓
Wait 48 hours
↓
Claim Child Benefit (you'll need the birth certificate)
↓
Report the new baby to Universal Credit → child element added to your payments
```

Would you like help finding your nearest register office, or more detail on any of the next steps?

💬 Response (10,490 in / 649 out)
Here's everything you need to know:

---

## 📋 How to Register the Birth

### Where & How
- **In person only** — you must visit your **local register office** (you cannot do this online or by phone)
- Find your nearest office: 👉 [gov.uk/register-offices](https://www.gov.uk/register-offices)

### What to Bring
- **Hospital notification of birth** (or midwife's notification if you had a home birth)
- **Both parents' ID documents**

### Key Decisions to Make Beforehand
- 🍼 **Have your baby's name decided** — it goes on the certificate
- 👨‍👩‍👧 **Will both parents be named?** If you're not married, both parents need to attend together to have both names on the certificate, or the father can complete a statutory declaration

### Deadline
- ⚠️ **42 days from the date of birth** — don't leave it too late, as hospitals can get busy and register offices may need appointments

### Contact
- 📞 **0300 123 1837** (Mon–Fri 8am–8pm, Sat 9am–4pm)

---

## 🔓 What Registering Unlocks

Once done, you can immediately move on to:

| Next Step | Why It Matters |
|---|---|
| **Child Benefit** | Regular payments — backdatable, so claim quickly |
| **Update Universal Credit** | Add the child element to your claim |
| **Sure Start Maternity Grant** | If you haven't already claimed the £500 grant |
| **Statutory Paternity Pay** | Your partner can finalise their paternity arrangements |

---

So as soon as your baby arrives, book your register office appointment straight away — and bring your baby's name ready! Would you like help with what to do immediately after registration, such as claiming Child Benefit or updating your Universal Credit?

💬 Response (16,288 in / 2,002 out)
I'm so sorry for your loss. Here is a clear, step-by-step guide to everything you need to do, based on the official GOV.UK guidance. Because your mother owned her home, there are some important extra steps around probate and inheritance tax.

---

## What to Do After Your Mother's Death — Step by Step

---

### ✅ STEP 1: Register the Death *(Must be done first — within 5 days)*
**Service:** [Register a death](https://www.gov.uk/register-a-death)

- You must register the death at a **local register office** — ideally in the area where she died.
- You'll need a **Medical Certificate of Cause of Death** from her doctor or hospital.
- ⚠️ If the death was unexpected or the cause is unclear, it may be **referred to a coroner** first — you cannot register until the coroner releases the case.
- Once registered, you'll receive the **Death Certificate** (get several certified copies — banks, insurers and solicitors will all want one) and a **unique Tell Us Once reference number**.

---

### ✅ STEP 2: Use "Tell Us Once" to Notify Government *(Within 28 days of registration)*
**Service:** [Tell Us Once](https://www.gov.uk/tell-us-once)

This single service notifies multiple government departments in one go, including:
- **HMRC** (to cancel tax credits, deal with personal tax)
- **DWP** (to cancel State Pension, Universal Credit, or other benefits)
- **DVLA** (driving licence)
- **Passport Office**
- **Local council** (Council Tax, Housing Benefit, Blue Badge, etc.)

You can do this online or by phone using the reference number from the registrar. You must use it **within 28 days**.

---

### ✅ STEP 3: Arrange the Funeral
**Service:** [Arrange a funeral](https://www.gov.uk/after-a-death/arrange-the-funeral)

- This can happen alongside Step 2.
- If you're struggling with costs, check if you're eligible for a [Funeral Expenses Payment](https://www.gov.uk/funeral-payments) from the government.

---

### ✅ STEP 4: Value the Estate (Including the Property) *(Before applying for probate)*
**Service:** [How to value an estate for Inheritance Tax](https://www.gov.uk/valuing-estate-of-someone-who-died)

Because your mother owned her home, this step is particularly important. You need to:
- **Identify all assets and debts** — property, savings, investments, pensions, any outstanding loans or mortgages.
- **Get the property professionally valued** (an estate agent or RICS surveyor can do this).
- **Estimate the total estate value** to determine if Inheritance Tax (IHT) is due.

**Inheritance Tax basics:**
- The standard IHT threshold is **£325,000**.
- There is an additional **Residence Nil Rate Band of £175,000** if the home is left to direct descendants (like you), making a potential total threshold of **£500,000**.
- IHT is charged at **40%** on the value above the threshold.
- If IHT is owed, you must report using **form IHT400** within **one year**, and begin paying within **6 months** of the death to avoid interest.
- ⚠️ **You cannot apply for probate until you have completed the estate valuation.**

---

### ✅ STEP 5: Apply for Probate *(After valuing the estate)*
**Service:** [Applying for probate](https://www.gov.uk/applying-for-probate)

Because your mother owned property in her sole name, **you will almost certainly need probate** before you can sell or transfer it.

- **If she left a will:** The named **executor(s)** apply for a **Grant of Probate**.
- **If she did not leave a will:** The closest next of kin (likely you) applies for **Letters of Administration**.
- You can apply **online or by post**.
- There is a court fee of **£300** for estates over £5,000 (free under £5,000).
- Contact the Courts and Tribunals Service Centre if you have questions: **0300 303 0648** (Mon–Fri, 9am–1pm) or email **contactprobate@justice.gov.uk**.

⚠️ **Do not put the property on the market or make financial plans until probate is granted.**

---

### ✅ STEP 6: Deal with the Estate *(After probate is granted)*
**Service:** [Deal with the estate](https://www.gov.uk/dealing-with-the-estate-of-someone-who-died)

Once you have the Grant of Probate or Letters of Administration, you can:
- **Pay any debts** your mother owed.
- **Close bank accounts** and transfer funds.
- **Sell or transfer the property** — you'll also need to [update the property records at HM Land Registry](https://www.gov.uk/update-property-records-someone-dies).
- **Distribute the remaining estate** according to the will (or the rules of intestacy if there was no will).

---

### 📋 Also Don't Forget (Outside Government):
- Notify her **bank(s)** and any **building societies**
- Contact her **private pension providers** and **life insurers**
- Inform her **utility companies**, **landlord** (if applicable), and **GP surgery**
- Cancel any **direct debits and subscriptions**

---

### 🆘 Need Help?
- If the estate is complex, consider hiring a **solicitor** — especially given the property.
- [MoneyHelper](https://www.moneyhelper.org.uk) has free, impartial guidance on probate and estate administration.
- [Cruse Bereavement Support](https://www.cruse.org.uk) offers free emotional support: **0808 808 1677**.

---

The most time-sensitive tasks are **registering the death (within 5 days)** and **using Tell Us Once (within 28 days)**. Everything else — especially probate — takes longer but can be worked through steadily. Take care of yourself through this process. 💙

💬 Response (9,965 in / 1,340 out)
I'm truly sorry for your loss. Here is a clear, step-by-step guide to everything you need to deal with, in the right order. Given that your mother owned her home, there are some important legal and financial steps to be aware of.

---

## 🗂️ Your Bereavement Action Plan

### ✅ Phase 1 — Do These First (Gateway Steps)

---

#### 1. 📋 Register the Death — *within 5 days*
**[Register a death – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/register-a-death)**
You must do this at the local register office in the area where your mother died. You'll need a medical certificate of cause of death from her doctor or hospital. This unlocks almost everything else.

---

#### 2. 📄 Obtain Multiple Death Certificates
**[Order death certificates – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/order-copy-birth-death-marriage-certificate)**
Order **several certified copies** (typically 5–10) at the time of registration — they cost £11 each. Banks, insurers, pension providers, and the probate registry all require **original** copies. This is much cheaper than ordering them later.

---

#### 3. 📢 Use Tell Us Once
**[Tell Us Once – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/after-a-death/organisations-you-need-to-contact-and-tell-us-once)**
After registering the death, you'll be given a Tell Us Once reference number. Use it to notify up to **23 government organisations in one go**, including:
- HMRC
- DWP (to stop any benefits/pension)
- DVLA (to cancel her driving licence)
- Passport Office
- Her local council

This saves enormous time and effort.

---

#### 4. 🏠 Inheritance Tax — *check if applicable, pay within 6 months*
**[Inheritance Tax – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/inheritance-tax)**
Because your mother owned her home, her estate may be above the tax-free threshold. Key thresholds:
- **£325,000** standard nil-rate band
- **Up to £500,000** if the home passes to direct descendants (children/grandchildren) — this uses the "residence nil-rate band"

If the estate exceeds the relevant threshold, you must **file an IHT400 form and pay any tax due within 6 months of death** — and this must happen **before probate is granted**.

---

#### 5. 🏛️ Apply for Probate — *after death certificate & IHT sorted*
**[Apply for probate – GOV.UK](https://www.gov.uk/applying-for-probate)**
Since your mother owned property, **probate will almost certainly be required**. This gives you legal authority to deal with her estate (sell/transfer the house, access bank accounts, etc.).
- Costs **£273** for estates over £5,000
- Currently taking **16+ weeks** to process — apply as soon as you can
- You'll need the death certificate and, if IHT applies, confirmation it's been dealt with

---

### ⚠️ Also Check — Proactively Flagged Services

| Service | Relevant? | Details |
|---|---|---|
| **Funeral Expenses Payment** | If you're on a qualifying benefit (e.g. Universal Credit, Pension Credit) | Claim within 6 months of the funeral — [apply here](https://www.gov.uk/funeral-payments) |
| **Cancel her driving licence** | Yes | Handled automatically by Tell Us Once, or send her licence to DVLA |
| **Council Tax on her property** | Yes | Notify her local council — an empty property may get a discount or exemption while the estate is being settled |
| **Notify OPG if she had a Power of Attorney** | If applicable | Any registered LPA ceases on death — notify the [Office of the Public Guardian](https://www.gov.uk/power-of-attorney/end-a-poa) |

---

### 🔑 Key Deadlines at a Glance

| Task | Deadline |
|---|---|
| Register the death | **5 days** |
| Pay Inheritance Tax (if due) | **6 months from death** |
| Claim Funeral Expenses Payment | **6 months from funeral** |

---

### 💡 What to Tackle Next (Practical Tips)

Beyond government services, you'll also want to:
- **Contact her bank(s)** — they'll freeze accounts on notification; probate will be needed to release funds
- **Notify her mortgage lender** (if any) or, if owned outright, her home insurer (empty properties need special cover)
- **Contact utility providers, pension schemes, and any subscriptions**
- **Instruct a solicitor** if the estate is complex — they can handle probate and the property transfer

---

Would you like more detail on any of these steps — for example, how probate works with the property, or whether Inheritance Tax is likely to apply in your mother's case?

Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
The ordering is excellent — register death first, then Tell Us Once, then IHT assessment before probate application, with proactive/conditional services flagged separately; this correctly reflects real-world dependencies.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●

💬 Response (54,965 in / 2,100 out)
Yes — this does change things, in two important ways: it affects **Inheritance Tax reporting** and it adds a **separate legal process in Spain**. Here's a full breakdown of what's different:

---

## How Overseas Assets Change Things

---

### 💰 1. Inheritance Tax — Overseas Assets Are Included

Because your mother was **UK domiciled** (she lived in the UK), **HMRC treats her worldwide estate as taxable** — this means both the Spanish property and any overseas savings must be included in the estate valuation for UK Inheritance Tax purposes.

**What this means practically:**

- The Spanish property and overseas savings must be **professionally valued** (in GBP at the date of death) and added to the total estate value alongside her UK home and UK savings.
- This makes it **more likely** the estate will exceed the IHT thresholds (£325,000 standard, or up to £500,000 with the Residence Nil Rate Band if the UK home passes to you).
- You will almost certainly need to complete **form IHT400** (the full inheritance tax return) rather than the simpler process, because the estate includes foreign assets.
- You must also complete **[form IHT417 – Foreign Assets](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/inheritance-tax-foreign-assets-iht417)**, which is submitted alongside IHT400. This form requires you to give details of all foreign property and financial assets.

**Double taxation relief:**
- Spain will also charge its own **Spanish Inheritance Tax (Impuesto de Sucesiones)** on the Spanish property.
- The UK has a **double taxation agreement** with Spain, which means you may be able to offset Spanish tax paid against the UK IHT bill on those same assets, so you don't pay full tax twice. A solicitor or tax adviser experienced in cross-border estates can help you claim this relief correctly.

---

### 🏠 2. The Spanish Property — A Separate Legal Process in Spain

The UK Grant of Probate **does not automatically apply in Spain**. Spanish property must go through **Spanish inheritance law separately**, regardless of what a UK will says.

Here is what is typically required in Spain:

| Step | What's involved |
|---|---|
| **Apostille the UK death certificate** | The UK death certificate must be officially legalised for use abroad via an [Apostille](https://www.gov.uk/get-document-legalised), issued by the Foreign, Commonwealth & Development Office (FCDO). |
| **Translate documents** | All UK documents (death certificate, will if applicable) must be translated into Spanish by a certified translator. |
| **Obtain a Spanish NIE number** | As the heir, you'll likely need a Spanish tax identification number (NIE) to deal with Spanish authorities. |
| **Engage a Spanish *gestor* or solicitor (abogado)** | You will need a Spanish lawyer to handle the inheritance process locally. They will deal with the Spanish notary, land registry, and tax authorities. |
| **Pay Spanish Inheritance Tax** | This must be paid in Spain within **6 months** of the date of death (extensions may be possible). The rate varies by region — Andalucía, Valencia, etc. all have different rules. |
| **Transfer the property** | Once Spanish tax is paid, the property can be transferred into your name or sold via the Spanish notary system. |

⚠️ **Important:** The 6-month Spanish tax deadline runs from the date of death — so you should appoint a Spanish lawyer **as soon as possible**, even while UK probate is still in progress.

---

### 📋 Revised Order of Priority for You

Given the overseas assets, here's how your priority list changes:

1. ✅ **Register the death** (within 5 days) — as before
2. ✅ **Tell Us Once** (within 28 days) — as before
3. ✅ **Arrange the funeral** — as before
4. 🆕 **Appoint a Spanish lawyer urgently** — the 6-month Spanish IHT deadline is already running
5. ✅ **Get the death certificate Apostilled** via the [FCDO Legalisation Office](https://www.gov.uk/get-document-legalised) — needed for Spain
6. ✅ **Value the entire worldwide estate** — UK property, UK savings, Spanish property (in GBP), overseas savings
7. ✅ **Complete IHT400 + IHT417** and pay any UK IHT due (within 6 months)
8. ✅ **Apply for UK Probate** (after IHT reporting is done)
9. ✅ **Deal with the UK estate** — sell/transfer UK property, close accounts, distribute assets
10. ✅ **Deal with the Spanish estate** in parallel via your Spanish lawyer

---

### 🆘 Strong Recommendation: Get Professional Help

This is now a genuinely complex cross-border estate. Given the combination of:
- Two properties (UK + Spain)
- Overseas savings
- Two separate tax jurisdictions
- A double taxation treaty to navigate

...you should strongly consider hiring a **UK solicitor who specialises in international estates**, alongside a **Spanish abogado**. Some UK firms handle both sides. The [Society of Trust and Estate Practitioners (STEP)](https://www.step.org) can help you find a qualified specialist.

You can also call **HMRC's Inheritance Tax helpline** for UK tax questions:
📞 **0300 123 1072** — Monday to Friday, 9am to 5pm

I'm sorry you're having to navigate all of this at such a difficult time. Please don't hesitate to ask if you'd like more detail on any of these steps. 💙
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 5/5 · Accuracy: 4/5 · Sequencing: 5/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 5/5 ●●●●●
The response covers both essential services (FCO document legalisation/Apostille) and conditional services (IHT400 + IHT417), and goes well beyond the checklist by addressing Spanish inheritance processes, double taxation relief, NIE requirements, and professional referral pathways.
Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○
Service names, form numbers, and processes are accurately described; the double taxation treaty claim between UK and Spain is correctly noted, though the statement that the UK has a specific double taxation agreement with Spain covering inheritance tax should be noted as nuanced — the UK-Spain treaty covers income/capital gains but inheritance tax relief is typically via unilateral relief under UK law rather than a bilateral IHT treaty, which is a minor but meaningful distinction.
Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●
The revised priority list is logically ordered, correctly emphasising the urgency of appointing a Spanish lawyer due to the 6-month deadline, and properly placing the Apostille step before the Spanish process while running UK and Spanish processes in parallel.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear headings, a useful table for the Spanish process, a numbered priority list, and appropriate use of formatting to guide the reader through a complex cross-border situation.
Notable errors: The response implies a bilateral double taxation agreement between UK and Spain covers inheritance tax, but in practice the UK-Spain treaty covers income and capital gains tax; IHT double taxation relief for Spain is typically claimed via unilateral relief under IHTA 1984 s.159 rather than a bilateral treaty.

💬 Response (18,628 in / 1,394 out)
Yes, overseas assets do add meaningful complexity. Here's a clear breakdown of what changes and what you need to do additionally:

---

## 🌍 How Overseas Assets Change Things

### 1. 💷 UK Inheritance Tax Still Applies to Everything

Because your mother was **UK domiciled** (she lived in the UK), **HMRC taxes her entire worldwide estate** — including the Spanish property and her savings, regardless of where they're held.

This means:
- The **Spanish property must be included** in the estate valuation for UK IHT purposes
- Her **overseas savings** must also be declared and valued
- You'll need to **convert all foreign asset values to GBP** at the exchange rate on the date of death
- You will almost certainly need to submit the **full IHT400 form** (the detailed version), not the simplified IHT205

---

### 2. 🇪🇸 Spain Has Its Own Inheritance Tax Too

Spain levies its own **Impuesto de Sucesiones** (Inheritance and Gift Tax) on property located in Spain. This is separate from UK IHT and is handled entirely through the Spanish tax authorities.

Key points:
- The tax is charged on the **Spanish property specifically**
- Rates and allowances vary significantly by **Spanish region (Comunidad Autónoma)** — some regions are much more generous than others
- You will need a **Spanish NIE number** (foreigner identification number) to deal with Spanish authorities
- You'll likely need to appoint a **Spanish solicitor (gestor or abogado)** to handle this

> ⚠️ **Good news on double taxation:** The UK has arrangements to give you **credit against UK IHT** for any Spanish inheritance tax you pay on the same asset, so you shouldn't be taxed twice on the full value of the property.

---

### 3. 🏠 Transferring the Spanish Property Requires Spanish Probate

UK probate **does not automatically cover assets in Spain**. You will need a separate legal process in Spain:

- Obtain an **apostille-certified copy** of the UK death certificate (and have it officially translated into Spanish)
- Apply for a **"Certificado de Últimas Voluntades"** (Certificate of Last Wills) from the Spanish Ministry of Justice to check if your mother made a Spanish will
- If she had a Spanish will, it will be dealt with in Spain. If not, the UK will applies but must be formally recognised in Spain
- A Spanish notary (**Notario**) will handle the transfer of the property title

---

### 4. 💰 The Savings

- **UK-held savings**: Included in the UK estate as normal — banks will freeze accounts on notification and release funds once probate is granted
- **Spanish-held savings** (e.g. a Spanish bank account): These will need to be dealt with through Spanish legal channels, in addition to being declared to HMRC for UK IHT

---

## 📋 Updated Action Plan

| Step | Task | Where |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | Register the death & use Tell Us Once | UK |
| 2 | Value the **full worldwide estate** (UK + Spain) | UK + Spain |
| 3 | Submit **IHT400** to HMRC and pay any UK IHT due | UK (within 6 months) |
| 4 | Apply for **UK probate** | UK |
| 5 | Instruct a **Spanish solicitor** to handle Spanish IHT and property transfer | Spain |
| 6 | Obtain apostille of death certificate + Spanish translation | UK → Spain |
| 7 | Check for a Spanish will via **Certificado de Últimas Voluntades** | Spain |
| 8 | Transfer Spanish property title via **Spanish Notario** | Spain |

---

## 💡 Strong Recommendation: Get Professional Help

Given the cross-border complexity, I'd strongly recommend:
- A **UK solicitor** experienced in international estates (look for one with STEP qualification — Society of Trust and Estate Practitioners)
- A **Spanish abogado or gestor** to handle the Spanish side in parallel
- These two professionals should ideally communicate with each other to coordinate the IHT credit for double taxation

---

Would you like guidance on finding a STEP-qualified solicitor, or do you have questions about any specific part of this process — such as the IHT400, the Spanish NIE, or what happens if your mother had (or didn't have) a Spanish will?

Accuracy 4/5 ●●●●○

Sequencing 5/5 ●●●●●

Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●

Notable omissions: fco-document-legalisation (explicitly named as a GOV.UK service for apostille)
Notable errors: The characterisation of UK-Spain double taxation relief as 'arrangements' is slightly vague — there is no bilateral IHT treaty with Spain; relief is typically unilateral under UK domestic law (s159 IHTA 1984)

## What Happens to Your Mother's Pension Credit (and Related Benefits)?

---

### ⚠️ The Key Risk: Overpayments

Pension Credit **does not stop automatically** the moment someone dies. Payments may continue to arrive in her bank account for days or even weeks after her death. **Any payments received after the date of death are overpayments, and DWP can recover them from the estate.** This is why notifying DWP quickly is so important.

---

### ✅ How to Notify DWP — Two Routes

#### Route 1: Tell Us Once *(Recommended — easiest)*
As mentioned in our earlier conversation, **Tell Us Once** is the simplest way to notify DWP. When you use it after registering the death, it automatically informs:
- The **Pension Service** (which administers Pension Credit)
- The **State Pension** team
- Any other DWP benefits she was receiving

This is the **preferred route** and covers everything in one go.

#### Route 2: Contact the Pension Service Directly
If you need to act urgently before Tell Us Once is available (e.g. before you've registered the death), or if you want to confirm the notification has been received, you can contact the **Pension Service** directly:

📞 **0800 731 0469** (freephone)
- Monday to Friday, **8am to 6pm**
- Relay UK: **18001 then 0800 731 0469**
- Welsh language: **0800 731 0453**

They handle both **Pension Credit** and **State Pension** queries, including bereavement notifications.

---

### 💷 What Happens to Payments Already Made?

| Payment timing | What happens |
|---|---|
| **Payments up to and including the date of death** | These are legitimately owed and belong to the estate |
| **Payments made after the date of death** | These are overpayments — DWP will ask for them back from the estate |

- If payments were made into her bank account, the bank will typically freeze the account once notified of the death. DWP will then contact the estate to recover any overpaid amounts.
- **Do not spend money from her account** after the date of death — this could create complications when settling the estate.

---

### 🔗 What Other Benefits May Have Been Linked to Pension Credit?

Because your mother was receiving Pension Credit, she may also have been automatically entitled to several other benefits that will also need to be cancelled. These include:

- 🏠 **Council Tax Reduction** — notify her local council (Tell Us Once covers this)
- 📺 **Free TV Licence** (if she was 75+) — TV Licensing will be notified via Tell Us Once
- 🌡️ **Warm Home Discount** — administered by her energy supplier; notify them directly
- ❄️ **Cold Weather Payments** — these stop automatically with Pension Credit
- 🏥 **Free NHS dental treatment, glasses and hospital travel costs** — these entitlements end with Pension Credit

---

### 💰 Does Pension Credit Affect the Estate or Inheritance Tax?

Yes — in one important way. Because your mother was receiving **means-tested Pension Credit**, DWP will have been aware of her financial circumstances. However:

- Pension Credit itself is **not recoverable from the estate** (unlike some care costs)
- However, if DWP later discovers she had **undisclosed assets** (such as the Spanish property or savings) that should have reduced or ended her Pension Credit entitlement, they could investigate and potentially seek to recover past payments
- As executor, you are not personally liable, but it's worth being aware of this when dealing with the estate

---

### 📋 Summary: Your Action Points for Benefits

| Priority | Action | How |
|---|---|---|
| 🔴 **Urgent** | Register the death | Register office |
| 🔴 **Urgent** | Use Tell Us Once | Online/phone with registrar's reference |
| 🟡 **If needed before Tell Us Once** | Call Pension Service directly | 📞 0800 731 0469 |
| 🟡 **Separately** | Notify energy supplier (Warm Home Discount) | Contact supplier directly |
| 🟢 **When dealing with estate** | Check for any DWP overpayment letters | Respond promptly and repay from estate |

---

The good news is that **Tell Us Once really does handle most of this for you** — it's one of the most useful services available at this difficult time. Please don't hesitate to ask if you have any more questions. 💙

---

## 💷 What Happens to Your Mother's Pension Credit

### ⛔ It Stops Immediately on Death

Pension Credit **ceases on the date of death**. It is a personal benefit and cannot be transferred or inherited. There is nothing you need to apply for — it simply ends.

---

### ⚠️ You Must Notify DWP Promptly

**If you used Tell Us Once** when registering the death, DWP will be notified automatically — this is the easiest route and covers Pension Credit along with other benefits.

**If you haven't used Tell Us Once yet**, contact DWP directly:

> 📞 **Pension Service line: 0800 731 0469**
> Monday–Friday, 8am–6pm
> Textphone: 0800 169 0133
> Relay UK: 18001 then 0800 731 0469

---

### 💰 Overpayments — Be Aware

DWP will calculate whether any Pension Credit was paid **after the date of death** (e.g. if a payment went in after she passed). If so, **the estate will be asked to repay the overpaid amount**. Keep an eye out for any DWP letters addressed to the estate.

---

### 📬 Final Payment / Arrears

Conversely, if any Pension Credit was **owed but not yet paid** at the date of death, the estate may be entitled to receive that final payment. DWP will confirm this when notified.

---

### 🔗 Knock-On Effects — Benefits Pension Credit Was Unlocking

Because Pension Credit is a **gateway benefit**, your mother may have been receiving other entitlements linked to it. These will also stop. Check whether she was receiving any of the following, and notify the relevant providers:

| Benefit | Action Needed |
|---|---|
| **Winter Fuel Payment** | Stops automatically — notify via Tell Us Once |
| **Free TV Licence** (if aged 75+) | Notify TV Licensing directly |
| **Warm Home Discount** | Notify her energy supplier |
| **Cold Weather Payment** | Stops automatically |
| **Housing Benefit** (if applicable) | Notify her local council |

---

### 📋 Summary of Actions

1. ✅ **Notify DWP** via Tell Us Once (if not done already) or by calling 0800 731 0469
2. 👀 **Watch for overpayment letters** from DWP — respond promptly and repay from the estate if required
3. 📋 **Check for linked benefits** she may have been receiving and notify those providers too

💬 Response (17,791 in / 1,969 out)
I now have everything I need to give you a clear, step-by-step guide. Here's what you should do, in order of priority:

---

## Your Action Plan After Redundancy

---

### ✅ Step 1 — Check Your Statutory Redundancy Pay (Do This Now)

Because you worked for the same employer for **5 years**, you are entitled to **Statutory Redundancy Pay**. You have **6 months from the date your job ends** to claim it.

The amount depends on your age and weekly pay. Use the official calculator now:
👉 [Calculate your statutory redundancy pay](https://www.gov.uk/calculate-your-redundancy-pay)

> **Important:** Your employer should pay this directly. If they refuse or can't pay (e.g. they're insolvent), you can claim from the government's National Insurance Fund via the [Redundancy Payments Service](https://www.gov.uk/redundancy-your-rights).

You are also entitled to a **notice period** (or pay in lieu of notice) and any **accrued holiday pay** — make sure your employer has paid these too.

---

### ✅ Step 2 — Claim New Style Jobseeker's Allowance (JSA) Immediately

Because you've been employed and paying National Insurance for 5 years, you should be eligible for **New Style JSA**, which is based on your NI contributions — **not** your savings or household income.

- **Amount:** Up to **£92.05/week** (if aged 25+)
- **Duration:** Up to **6 months (182 days)**
- You must be actively looking for work
- **Claim as soon as possible** — payments don't backdate, and there can be a wait of up to 3 weeks for your first payment

👉 [How to claim New Style JSA](https://www.gov.uk/how-to-claim-new-style-jsa)

---

### ✅ Step 3 — Claim Universal Credit (UC) at the Same Time

You can claim **Universal Credit alongside New Style JSA** to top up your income. UC is a means-tested benefit that covers living costs and can also help with housing costs. Since you have no savings, you are likely to qualify.

- **Note:** UC is paid **monthly**, and your first payment typically takes **5 weeks** — so claim as soon as possible
- You can request a **UC Advance Payment** if you need money urgently while you wait
- Your redundancy pay may affect your UC entitlement temporarily (it may be treated as capital/income), so declare it when you apply

👉 [Claim Universal Credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit/how-to-claim)

---

### ✅ Step 4 — Apply for Support for Mortgage Interest (SMI)

Because you have a mortgage and no savings, this is critical. **SMI** is a government loan that pays the **interest on your mortgage** while you're receiving a qualifying benefit (such as Universal Credit or New Style JSA).

- It is paid as a **loan**, repaid (with interest) when you eventually sell or transfer your home — **not** from your monthly income
- It does **not** cover missed payments (arrears) or capital repayments
- You become eligible once you're receiving a qualifying benefit — **this is why claiming UC/JSA first (Step 2 & 3) is essential**

👉 [Support for Mortgage Interest (SMI)](https://www.gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest)

> 💡 **Also contact your mortgage lender now** — before you miss any payments. Many lenders offer a **payment holiday**, a switch to **interest-only payments**, or an extended term during hardship. This is separate from SMI and can provide immediate relief.

---

### ✅ Step 5 — Start Looking for Work via the Find a Job Service

Your JSA and UC claims will require you to actively seek work. Use the government's official job search tool:
👉 [Find a Job](https://www.gov.uk/find-a-job)

Your **Jobcentre Plus work coach** (assigned when you claim JSA/UC) can also help with CVs, interview prep, and training opportunities.

---

💬 Response (16,112 in / 1,572 out)
Here's a clear, prioritised plan for your situation. Given you have a mortgage and no savings, I've focused on the most urgent steps first.

---

## 🚨 Phase 1 — Do These Right Away

### 1. 📄 Get Your P45 from Your Employer
Your employer is **legally required** to give you a P45 on or after your last day. You'll need it to claim benefits and get any tax refund. If they haven't sent it, chase them immediately.
👉 [gov.uk/paye-forms-p45-p60-p11d/p45](https://www.gov.uk/paye-forms-p45-p60-p11d/p45)

---

### 2. 💷 Claim Statutory Redundancy Pay
With **5 years' service**, you are entitled to statutory redundancy pay. The amount depends on your age and weekly pay (capped at £643/week). Your employer must pay this — if they refuse, you can take them to an Employment Tribunal.
👉 [gov.uk/redundancy-payments-helpline](https://www.gov.uk/redundancy-payments-helpline)

---

### 3. 🏠 Apply for Universal Credit (UC) — **Do This Now**
As you have no savings and a mortgage, UC is your main financial lifeline. It covers living costs and, crucially for you, it **unlocks Support for Mortgage Interest** (see below). Apply as soon as possible — payments take about 5 weeks to arrive.
👉 [gov.uk/universal-credit](https://www.gov.uk/universal-credit)

---

### 4. 🏡 Support for Mortgage Interest (SMI) — **Critical for You**
Because you have a mortgage and no savings, this is especially important. Once you're on UC, you can apply for an **SMI loan** — a government loan (secured against your property) that covers your mortgage interest payments while you're out of work. It prevents you falling into arrears.
👉 [gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest](https://www.gov.uk/support-for-mortgage-interest)

> ⚠️ **Also contact your mortgage lender directly** to ask about a mortgage payment holiday or reduced payments — lenders are required to consider this.

---

### 5. 💼 New Style Jobseeker's Allowance (JSA)
Because you've been employed for 5 years, you've almost certainly built up enough **National Insurance contributions** to claim New Style JSA. This pays up to **£84.80/week for 6 months** and can be claimed *on top of* Universal Credit. Apply at the same time as UC.
👉 [gov.uk/jobseekers-allowance/new-style-jsa](https://www.gov.uk/jobseekers-allowance/new-style-jsa)

---

### 6. 🏛️ Council Tax Reduction
Apply to your **local council** for a reduction on your council tax bill. On a very low income, this can reduce your bill to zero. Apply as soon as you've applied for UC.
👉 [gov.uk/apply-council-tax-reduction](https://www.gov.uk/apply-council-tax-reduction)

---

### 7. 💰 Income Tax Refund (P50)
Since you've lost your job mid-tax year and may not return to work before 5 April, you've likely **overpaid PAYE tax** and are owed a refund. Claim this from HMRC — it could be a useful lump sum.
👉 [gov.uk/claim-tax-refund](https://www.gov.uk/claim-tax-refund/you-get-a-pension)

---

### 8. 🛡️ Breathing Space (Debt Respite Scheme)
If you're worried about bills and debt piling up while you wait for benefits to kick in, you can apply (through a free debt adviser) for **60 days of legal protection** from creditor action — no interest, no enforcement, no chasing. Contact **StepChange** or **Citizens Advice** to apply.
👉 [gov.uk/options-for-dealing-with-your-debts/breathing-space](https://www.gov.uk/options-for-dealing-with-your-debts/breathing-space)

---

### 9. 🔍 Start Job Searching
DWP's free job search service lists thousands of vacancies across the UK.
👉 [gov.uk/find-a-job](https://www.gov.uk/find-a-job)

---

## 📋 Also Worth Knowing

| Service | What it does |
|---|---|
| **NI Credits** | Automatically protect your State Pension record while you're on UC/JSA — no action needed |
| **NHS Low Income Scheme (HC2)** | Free/reduced prescriptions, dental and sight tests if your income is low |
| **Social Tariff Broadband** | Discounted broadband (~£12–20/month) once you're on UC |
| **ACAS Early Conciliation** | If you believe your redundancy was unfair, contact ACAS within **3 months minus 1 day** — it's free and required before any tribunal claim |

6. **Apply for Council Tax Reduction** at your local council

💬 Response (11,268 in / 1,312 out)
Yes — being signed off by your GP changes your situation meaningfully. Here's exactly how:

---

## How Being Signed Off Affects Your Plan

---

### 🔄 Key Change: Swap New Style JSA → New Style ESA

Being signed off with stress and anxiety means you **cannot claim New Style JSA**, because JSA requires you to be **actively available for and seeking work**. If you're too unwell to work, you don't meet that condition.

Instead, you should claim **New Style Employment and Support Allowance (ESA)**, which is specifically designed for people who **cannot work due to illness or a health condition** — including mental health conditions like stress and anxiety.

---

### ✅ New Step 2 (Revised) — Claim New Style ESA Immediately

**New Style ESA** is a contributory benefit based on your National Insurance record. With 5 years of employment, you should have more than enough NI contributions to qualify.

- 💰 **Amount:** Up to **£92.05/week** initially (same as JSA), potentially rising after assessment
- 📋 You will need to provide your **GP's fit note (sick note)** as part of your claim
- 🏥 After an initial period, you'll be asked to undergo a **Work Capability Assessment (WCA)** — a medical assessment to determine how your condition affects your ability to work
- The WCA places you in one of two groups:
- **Work-Related Activity Group (WRAG):** You may be able to work in the future; some requirements to prepare for work
- **Support Group:** Your condition is more severe; no work-related requirements at all
- Your savings and partner's income do **not** affect New Style ESA

👉 [Claim New Style ESA](https://www.gov.uk/guidance/new-style-employment-and-support-allowance)

> ⚠️ **Claim as soon as possible.** There is a **7-day waiting period** at the start of a claim (the first 7 days are not paid), and payments are not backdated beyond your claim date.

---

### ✅ Step 3 Remains — Also Claim Universal Credit

You should **still claim Universal Credit alongside New Style ESA**. Because you have a health condition, UC can include an **additional amount** on top of the standard allowance once your Work Capability Assessment is completed.

- Report your health condition when you apply for UC
- Submit your fit note through your UC journal
- Your UC work-search requirements will be **reduced or removed entirely** while you are signed off — you won't be expected to look for jobs while unwell
- Any New Style ESA you receive will be taken into account, but you may still receive a UC top-up

👉 [Health conditions and Universal Credit](https://www.gov.uk/health-conditions-disability-universal-credit)

---

### ✅ Steps 1, 4 & 5 Remain Unchanged

| Step | Action | Status |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | Claim your statutory redundancy pay | ✅ Unchanged |
| 2 | ~~Claim New Style JSA~~ → **Claim New Style ESA** | 🔄 **Updated** |
| 3 | Claim Universal Credit (report your health condition) | ✅ Still applies, with extra protections |
| 4 | Contact your mortgage lender + apply for SMI once on benefits | ✅ Unchanged |
| 5 | ~~Job searching~~ — not required while signed off | 🔄 **Paused while unwell** |

---

### 💡 A Few Extra Tips Given Your Mental Health

- **Keep renewing your fit note** — your GP will need to issue these regularly to keep your ESA and UC health protections in place
- The **Work Capability Assessment** can feel daunting — Citizens Advice can help you prepare and even accompany you
- **Mind** (mind.org.uk) and **Samaritans** (116 123) offer free mental health support if the financial stress is adding to your anxiety
- Make sure your GP knows about the financial pressures you're under — it's all relevant to your health record

---

Would you like more detail on the Work Capability Assessment process, or anything else?

💬 Response (33,130 in / 1,421 out)
Yes — being signed off with stress and anxiety changes things significantly, and in some ways works in your favour financially. Here's what's new or updated:

---

## 🆕 What Changes Because of Your GP Sign-Off

### 1. 🔄 New Style ESA Instead of (or as well as) JSA — **Consider Switching**
Because you're signed off as unfit for work, **New Style Employment & Support Allowance (ESA)** may be more appropriate than New Style JSA. ESA is specifically for people whose health prevents them from working. Like JSA, it's based on your NI contributions and pays a similar rate — but it doesn't require you to actively job-search while you're signed off.

> ⚠️ You generally can't claim both JSA and ESA at the same time. Given your GP has signed you off, ESA is likely the right choice. Discuss this with DWP when you call.
👉 [gov.uk/employment-support-allowance](https://www.gov.uk/employment-support-allowance/eligibility)

---

### 2. 💰 UC Health Element (LCWRA) — Up to **£423/month extra**
This is a big one for you. Because you're on UC **and** have a health condition limiting your ability to work, you can be assessed for the **Limited Capability for Work and Work-Related Activity (LCWRA)** element. If awarded, this adds **£423.27/month** on top of your standard UC payment.

**How it works:**
- Submit your **fit note** to DWP via your UC online journal as soon as you apply
- DWP will send you a **UC50 questionnaire** about how your condition affects you
- You'll then have a **Work Capability Assessment (WCA)** with a health professional
- If found to have LCWRA, the extra payment is backdated to when you submitted your fit note

> 💡 **Keep getting fit notes from your GP** — you'll need them continuously until the WCA is completed.

---

### 3. 🏥 Personal Independence Payment (PIP) — **Worth Applying For**
PIP is **not means-tested** and is paid regardless of whether you're working or claiming other benefits. It's for people with a long-term health condition affecting daily life. Stress and anxiety absolutely can qualify — what matters is how your condition affects you day-to-day (washing, dressing, cooking, concentrating, managing medication, going out).

**Key rules:**
- You must have had difficulties for **at least 3 months** and expect them to last **at least 9 more months**
- Rates range from **£29.20 to £110.40/week** depending on your needs
- It's worth applying now if your condition is ongoing — the 3-month qualifying period starts from when difficulties began, not when you apply

📞 PIP helpline: **0800 917 2222** (Mon–Fri, 8am–5pm)
👉 [gov.uk/pip](https://www.gov.uk/pip)

> 💡 **PIP also unlocks further benefits** — including free prescriptions, a Disabled Persons Railcard, and potentially a Council Tax Disability Reduction.

---

### 4. 📋 Send Your Fit Note to DWP
Once you've applied for UC and/or ESA, you'll need to **submit your fit note digitally** to DWP. You can do this through your UC online journal.
👉 [gov.uk/send-fit-note](https://www.gov.uk/send-fit-note)

---

### 5. 🧠 Access to Work — Mental Health Support
If and when you return to work, **Access to Work** can fund mental health support in the workplace — including counselling, coaching, and workplace adjustments. Keep this in mind for when you're ready to return.
👉 [gov.uk/access-to-work](https://www.gov.uk/access-to-work)

---

## 📋 Updated Priority Order for You

| Priority | Action |
|---|---|
| 1 | Get your **fit note** from your GP (you likely already have it) |
| 2 | Apply for **Universal Credit** today |
| 3 | Apply for **New Style ESA** (instead of JSA, given your sign-off) |
| 4 | **Submit your fit note** via your UC journal immediately |
| 5 | Complete the **UC50 questionnaire** when DWP sends it |
| 6 | Consider applying for **PIP** if your condition has lasted/will last 12+ months |
| 7 | Apply for **Support for Mortgage Interest** once UC is confirmed |
| 8 | Apply for **Council Tax Reduction** at your local council |

---

💬 Response (30,568 in / 1,431 out)
I now have everything I need. Here's a clear breakdown of how your £12,000 redundancy payment affects each benefit:

---

## How Your £12,000 Redundancy Payment Affects Your Benefits

---

### ✅ New Style ESA — **Not affected at all**

Good news here. New Style ESA is based entirely on your **National Insurance contributions record**, not your savings or capital. Your £12,000 redundancy payment has **absolutely no effect** on your eligibility or the amount you receive.

> **Claim this regardless of your redundancy pay.**

---

### ⚠️ Universal Credit — **Significantly affected**

This is where your £12,000 matters most. UC has strict capital (savings) rules:

| Your savings/capital | Effect on UC |
|---|---|
| **Under £6,000** | No effect on UC payments |
| **£6,000 – £16,000** | UC is **reduced** — DWP assumes you earn a "tariff income" of **£4.35/month for every £250 (or part thereof) above £6,000** |
| **Over £16,000** | You are **not eligible for UC at all** |

Your £12,000 redundancy payment puts you **in the middle band** (£6,000–£16,000). This means:

- You **can still claim UC** ✅
- But your UC payment will be **reduced** as if you have a notional income from that capital
- The excess above £6,000 is **£6,000** → that's 24 lots of £250 → **£4.35 × 24 = ~£104/month deducted** from your UC
- As your savings reduce (e.g. from paying your mortgage), your UC payments will **gradually increase** — you must report changes in your capital to DWP

> ⚠️ **Important:** You must **declare your redundancy payment** when you apply for UC. Failing to do so could be treated as fraud.

---

### 💡 Does the Redundancy Pay Count Immediately?

Yes — DWP will treat the £12,000 as capital **from the date you receive it**. However, it's worth knowing:

- **Statutory Redundancy Pay** is included in the capital calculation
- Money you spend on **reasonable living expenses** (mortgage, food, bills) will reduce your capital over time, and your UC will adjust accordingly each monthly assessment period
- You should **not** deliberately spend money down to reduce your capital artificially — DWP can apply "deprivation of capital" rules if they believe you've done this to increase your benefits

---

### ✅ Support for Mortgage Interest (SMI) — **Not directly affected**

SMI eligibility is linked to receiving a qualifying benefit (UC or ESA), not to your savings level. Since you can still claim both New Style ESA and UC, you remain eligible for SMI once those claims are in place.

---

## Updated Summary

| Benefit | Affected by £12,000? | Action |
|---|---|---|
| **New Style ESA** | ❌ No effect | Claim immediately |
| **Universal Credit** | ⚠️ Reduced by ~£104/month | Still claim — declare the payment honestly |
| **SMI** | ❌ No direct effect | Apply once ESA/UC are in place |

---

### 💡 One More Thing — Tax on Redundancy Pay

The first **£30,000 of redundancy pay is tax-free**, so your full £12,000 is **completely free of income tax and National Insurance**. You don't need to do anything about this — your employer should have paid it without deducting tax.

---

> 💬 Given the complexity of the capital rules, it's worth speaking to **Citizens Advice** or using the **Turn2Us benefits calculator** (turn2us.org.uk) to get a precise figure for what UC you're entitled to right now.

Would you like help with anything else, such as what to say to your mortgage lender or how to prepare for your Work Capability Assessment?

💬 Response (14,242 in / 1,184 out)
Good news — the picture is more nuanced than a simple "yes it counts against you." Here's exactly how your £12,000 redundancy payment affects each benefit:

---

## 💷 How Your £12,000 Redundancy Pay Affects Each Benefit

### ✅ New Style ESA — **Not Affected At All**
New Style ESA is **contribution-based**, not means-tested. Your redundancy payment, savings, and other income are **completely ignored**. You qualify based solely on your NI record and your fit note. This is unaffected.

> 📞 ESA helpline: **0800 055 6688** (Mon–Fri, 8am–6pm)

---

### ✅ PIP — **Not Affected At All**
PIP is also **not means-tested**. Your redundancy payment is entirely irrelevant to your PIP claim. It's based purely on how your condition affects your daily life.

> 📞 PIP helpline: **0800 917 2222** (Mon–Fri, 8am–5pm)

---

### ⚠️ Universal Credit — **Affected, But You Still Qualify**
UC *is* means-tested, and your redundancy pay counts as **capital (savings)**. Here's how the rules work:

| Your Capital | Effect on UC |
|---|---|
| Under £6,000 | No effect — full UC paid |
| £6,000–£16,000 | **Tariff income applied** — UC reduced by £4.35/month for every £250 (or part thereof) above £6,000 |
| Over £16,000 | UC not payable at all |

**Your situation:** £12,000 is £6,000 above the lower threshold.
- That's 24 × £250 bands = **£104.40/month deducted** from your UC payment
- You still qualify — you're well under the £16,000 cut-off
- **Crucially**, as you spend the money on living costs, your capital falls — and your UC payments will increase accordingly each month

> 💡 **Important:** Statutory redundancy pay itself is **disregarded** for the first assessment period in some cases, but the money sitting in your bank account counts as capital from the point you receive it. Report it honestly when you apply.

> ⚠️ **Deprivation of capital rule:** Don't be tempted to spend it quickly just to get more UC — DWP can treat you as still having money you've deliberately spent down.

---

### ✅ Support for Mortgage Interest — **Not Affected**
SMI is linked to your UC claim, not your capital level. As long as you're on UC, you can apply for SMI regardless of your redundancy payment.

---

### ✅ Council Tax Reduction — **Varies by Council**
Most councils use a similar capital threshold to UC (often £6,000–£16,000), so you may see a small reduction in your CTR award too. Check with your local council — but you will still receive *some* reduction.

---

## 📊 The Bigger Picture

Here's the key takeaway:

| Benefit | Affected by £12k? | Still Eligible? |
|---|---|---|
| New Style ESA | ❌ No | ✅ Yes — full amount |
| PIP | ❌ No | ✅ Yes — full amount |
| Universal Credit | ⚠️ Slightly reduced | ✅ Yes — ~£104/month less |
| Support for Mortgage Interest | ❌ No | ✅ Yes (once on UC) |
| Council Tax Reduction | ⚠️ Possibly slightly reduced | ✅ Likely yes |

---

## 💡 One Practical Tip

Your £12,000 is a genuine financial cushion — but **don't delay applying for UC**. The 5-week wait for your first payment starts from the day you apply, not the day your money runs out. Apply now, declare the redundancy payment honestly, and let your UC payments increase naturally as your capital reduces.

Would you like help estimating your likely monthly income across all these benefits combined?
⚖️ Judge scores — Completeness: 4/5 · Accuracy: 3/5 · Sequencing: 4/5 · Clarity: 5/5
Completeness 4/5 ●●●●○
The response covers UC (essential) and mentions several other benefits, but notably omits New Style JSA, which is the most directly relevant conditional service for someone made redundant, while including ESA and PIP which the citizen didn't ask about.
Accuracy 3/5 ●●●○○
The UC capital rules and tariff income calculation are broadly correct, but the tariff income figure of £4.35/month per £250 band is incorrect (it should be £4.35 per week, not per month, meaning the monthly deduction would be closer to £104 per week × ~4.33 ≈ significantly more); also the claim that statutory redundancy pay is 'disregarded for the first assessment period in some cases' is not a standard UC rule and is misleading.
Sequencing 4/5 ●●●●○
The advice to apply for UC promptly and not delay is well-placed and practical, though the response could have led with UC and JSA as the most immediately relevant benefits rather than ESA and PIP.
Clarity 5/5 ●●●●●
The response is exceptionally well-structured with clear tables, headings, and practical tips that make it very easy to follow and understand.
Notable omissions: New Style JSA
Notable errors: Tariff income calculation appears to conflate weekly and monthly figures — £4.35 is per week per £250 band, not per month, making the actual monthly deduction approximately £104 per week not per month, The claim about statutory redundancy pay being disregarded for the first assessment period is not an established UC rule, ESA and PIP are discussed at length despite no indication the citizen has a health condition
